  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_3029dfd0-bc82-473a-8a39-e8bb188ff2c1'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer699120119337562540">When John Rechy's explosive first novel, <i>City of Night</i>, was first published in 1963, it became a national bestseller and ushered in a new era of gay fiction. Bold and inventive in his account of the urban underworld of male prostitution, Rechy is equally unflinching in his portrayal of one hustling "Youngman" and his restless search for self-knowledge. As the narrator care</span>
  <span id="freeText699120119337562540" style="display:none">When John Rechy's explosive first novel, <i>City of Night</i>, was first published in 1963, it became a national bestseller and ushered in a new era of gay fiction. Bold and inventive in his account of the urban underworld of male prostitution, Rechy is equally unflinching in his portrayal of one hustling "Youngman" and his restless search for self-knowledge. As the narrator careens from El Paso to Times Square, from Pershing Square to the French Quarter, we get an unforgettable look at a neon-lit life on the edge. Said James Baldwin of the author, "Rechy is the most arresting young writer I've read in a very long time. His tone rings absolutely true, is absolutely his own; and he has the kind of discipline which allows him a rare and beautiful reckless."</span>
  <a data-text-id="699120119337562540" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1277739428" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1277739428" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32804270-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564311252p2/32804270.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1277739428?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/32804270-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=recs">recs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1277739428" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer547589644179572687">Expansive and electric, <i>City of Night</i> brings to life a young unnamed hustler’s coming of age. The autobiographical story follows the Texas-born protagonist as he treks across the country, from his hometown of El Paso to New York, Los Angeles, San Francisco, and New Orleans, meeting an eclectic bunch of outcasts, drag queens, and fellow sex workers along the way. The novel’s anthropological in its documentation of gay and trans social life; its chapters alternate between vivid portraits of cities</span>
  <span id="freeText547589644179572687" style="display:none">Expansive and electric, <i>City of Night</i> brings to life a young unnamed hustler’s coming of age. The autobiographical story follows the Texas-born protagonist as he treks across the country, from his hometown of El Paso to New York, Los Angeles, San Francisco, and New Orleans, meeting an eclectic bunch of outcasts, drag queens, and fellow sex workers along the way. The novel’s anthropological in its documentation of gay and trans social life; its chapters alternate between vivid portraits of cities and incisive sketches of social types. Unlike the terse style of <i>The Sexual Outlaw</i>, Rechy’s prose here is baroque and breathtaking, and the author explores his themes with a greater sense of apprehension. At the core of the novel is the question of whether monogamy, or any kind of love, is possible for those forced to live and bond on the margins of society. Rechy doesn’t try to answer that question, but his consideration of it is brilliant. Well worth checking out.</span>
  <a data-text-id="547589644179572687" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/775916963" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/775916963?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/1047783-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/9745528-city-of-night">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer775916963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7625572267227356670">I just saw in the NYTimes that Grove is putting out the 50th anniversary edition - my heart stopped for just a second, and even as I'm writing this my stomach has that forbidden fruit feeling of something thrilling and frightening this way coming. (It's the same feeling I got well into my adult years when driving into NYC - an-tici-pation.)<br/><br/>In 1963 I was a 17 years old and a totally alienated wanna-be hipster/beatnik reaching out for anything dark and maybe beautiful. I saw the American dream as </span>
  <span id="freeText7625572267227356670" style="display:none">I just saw in the NYTimes that Grove is putting out the 50th anniversary edition - my heart stopped for just a second, and even as I'm writing this my stomach has that forbidden fruit feeling of something thrilling and frightening this way coming. (It's the same feeling I got well into my adult years when driving into NYC - an-tici-pation.)<br /><br />In 1963 I was a 17 years old and a totally alienated wanna-be hipster/beatnik reaching out for anything dark and maybe beautiful. I saw the American dream as a klieg-lit freak show, and wanted nothing more than the shadows. City of Night was a primer, and a baby step into the world I was actually going to find myself in a few years later. <br /><br />I don't know how this book holds up, but it gets its five stars for the impact it had on me way back then. I'm glad to see it's still available, and amazed that its reissue has caused me such a visceral reaction on this fog-bound, living-room comfortable, cozy and warm Thanksgiving morning.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7625572267227356670" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/112493580?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J" href="/user/show/1021324-j">J</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer112493580" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8883841236273088620">Truly gripping and evocative. The ending was so incredibly touching. The book is filled with moments that perfectly capture the alienated gay culture of the 60s in an at times shocking way. In addition to the sullen and often mellow persona of John's personality, there are also moments punctuated here where drag queens just bring it ON. Colorful personalities bloom everywhere around him. This book is made of awesome and the prose is nothing less than gorgeous. A lot of this reminded me of Jack K</span>
  <span id="freeText8883841236273088620" style="display:none">Truly gripping and evocative. The ending was so incredibly touching. The book is filled with moments that perfectly capture the alienated gay culture of the 60s in an at times shocking way. In addition to the sullen and often mellow persona of John's personality, there are also moments punctuated here where drag queens just bring it ON. Colorful personalities bloom everywhere around him. This book is made of awesome and the prose is nothing less than gorgeous. A lot of this reminded me of Jack Kerouac, if he was more refined and could concentrate better. This is more streamlined than Kerouac's work, and though both shared a love for the scope of the American road and cityscapes, this is a memorable book for being one of the first to openly depict the often over or underlooked gay scene that was so consistently marginalized. So when the gushing honesty of its characters overflows onto the page, do not be surprised to find yourself in awe of what this book actually accomplishes.</span>

<div id="review_14335123" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14335123" />
    <a title="Raymond" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/679823-raymond"><img alt="Raymond" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1197501464p2/679823.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14335123?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raymond" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raymond" href="/user/show/679823-raymond">Raymond</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/679823-raymond?shelf=literary-poetry">literary-poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/679823-raymond?shelf=gay-interest">gay-interest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/679823-raymond?shelf=rechy-john">rechy-john</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14335123" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1977380216092282704">City of Night, as I remember it, is a powerful, dead on depiction of the gay underworld of the late 50's, early sixties. For a young gay man, and occasional trick turner, it was a book that spoke to my experience in a world that did not want me to be. <br/><br/>There is a particular scene in the book that stays with me still. During a Mardi Gras celebration, the protagonist (we never know his name) leaps on to a float carrying a beautiful young drag queen (Kathy, and her hustler lover (Jocko) and asks Kat</span>
  <span id="freeText1977380216092282704" style="display:none">City of Night, as I remember it, is a powerful, dead on depiction of the gay underworld of the late 50's, early sixties. For a young gay man, and occasional trick turner, it was a book that spoke to my experience in a world that did not want me to be. <br /><br />There is a particular scene in the book that stays with me still. During a Mardi Gras celebration, the protagonist (we never know his name) leaps on to a float carrying a beautiful young drag queen (Kathy, and her hustler lover (Jocko) and asks Kathy why she is smiling.<br /><br />"Because I'm going to die", she says.<br /><br />I've never forgotten that particular moment in the book. The whole of the book is encapsuled in that book- the tawdry glitter, the desperation under the affirmation of self.<br /><br />I once met Mr. Rechy at gay fund raiser and tried to speak to him about the why of the story and the why of that particular scene. He was taciturn, almost rude. Later I decided it was that I was too gushing in my praise and it made him uneasy. Maybe he was just tired of explaining it to idiots. I was so young, then.<br /><br />One thing I did learn from the book (and from my own experiences) is that tricks and Johns need each other and yet are so often contemptuous of each other.<br /><br />This is a perfect book for those who have known only After Stonewall. It is a history of the shadows gay people had to wrap around themselves.<br /><br />I know that my words here are not a review, proper. I don't have the background to comment on pure literary merit. It is, however, what it is.<br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="1977380216092282704" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3099297804" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3099297804" />
    <a title="Kinga" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/110812-kinga"><img alt="Kinga" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491308194p2/110812.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3099297804?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kinga" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kinga" href="/user/show/110812-kinga">Kinga</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/110812-kinga?shelf=pub-1963">pub-1963</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3099297804" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1812652347218416246">I stumbled upon this gay cult classic accidentally and went into it without knowing its status or significance. Though, the latter became apparent as I read. <br/><br/>Published in 1963, it’s a picture of the underground gay culture pre-Stonewall, filled with excellent sociological observation and a cast of colourful characters (even if some of them become a little on the nose – I’m looking at you, Nazi masochist with daddy issues).<br/><br/>The character’s journey of wanting love but fearing it and running away fr</span>
  <span id="freeText1812652347218416246" style="display:none">I stumbled upon this gay cult classic accidentally and went into it without knowing its status or significance. Though, the latter became apparent as I read. <br /><br />Published in 1963, it’s a picture of the underground gay culture pre-Stonewall, filled with excellent sociological observation and a cast of colourful characters (even if some of them become a little on the nose – I’m looking at you, Nazi masochist with daddy issues).<br /><br />The character’s journey of wanting love but fearing it and running away from it wasn’t maybe as ground-breaking but his circumstances must’ve been a novelty for a 1960s reader (obviously those readers who didn’t live those circumstances themselves). The public loved the book, but many reviewers were condescending and treated the author as some idiot-savant, denying the book a true literary value that comes from careful consideration, or even straight-out denying the existence of John Rechy. <br /><br />The narrator of the book never admits to himself he is gay, and in self-delusion insists he only turns tricks for money, and like many others in his position, fully believes one day he will abandon this life and start a wholesome heterosexual existence – the only place where real love is possible. Meanwhile he performs the fantasy of masculinity for his clients. Of course, he keeps returning to this demimonde in every city he goes to – New York, Los Angeles, San Francisco, Chicago, New Orleans, but never allowing himself true intimacy with another human being. He reacts with anger and contempt for anyone who attempts to get close to him. <br /><br />The reader gets a glimmer of hope that the narrator can free himself from his all-consuming self-loathing and get a happy ending of sorts if only the post-orgasm shame could be overcome. At least we can comfort ourselves with the knowledge that the author of this semi-autobiographical novel found love and happiness in the end. <br /><br />Read this book if you’re tired of the polished, Mad Men-like vision of the 50s in America.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1812652347218416246" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2507259629" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2507259629" />
    <a title="ALLEN" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58158333-allen"><img alt="ALLEN" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578421145p2/58158333.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2507259629?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ALLEN" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ALLEN" href="/user/show/58158333-allen">ALLEN</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2507259629" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7813428983153852120">CITY OF NIGHT is what you get when you cross headlong, Beat Generation writing with a young man's emerging sense of self as gay -- and it's a shocking and wonderful meld indeed. This 1963 novel stunned readers when it first appeared: the narrator whose father's friends demanded he "give them a thousand," his youth as a hustler, the lurking, intense romanticism of various American "entertainment districts." The novel combines an understanding not only of the insistent drumbeats of gay sexuality a</span>
  <span id="freeText7813428983153852120" style="display:none">CITY OF NIGHT is what you get when you cross headlong, Beat Generation writing with a young man's emerging sense of self as gay -- and it's a shocking and wonderful meld indeed. This 1963 novel stunned readers when it first appeared: the narrator whose father's friends demanded he "give them a thousand," his youth as a hustler, the lurking, intense romanticism of various American "entertainment districts." The novel combines an understanding not only of the insistent drumbeats of gay sexuality and male emotion but also the orange-drink-and-popcorn-scented center of the seediest American cities, ca. 1960. It can be read as "gay lit," of course, or simply as Americana, hot and heavy. I think it succeeds on both counts. John Rechy went on to write many other books, most notably THE SEXUAL OUTLAW, but in my opinion this one is his best. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7813428983153852120" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_105648668" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/105648668" />
    <a title="David Gallagher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3456763-david-gallagher"><img alt="David Gallagher" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304117043p2/3456763.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/105648668?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Gallagher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Gallagher" href="/user/show/3456763-david-gallagher">David Gallagher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3456763-david-gallagher?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3456763-david-gallagher?shelf=read-in-2010">read-in-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer105648668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15442636149237090712">I wanted to like this book more - I really did. Having been born post the AIDS/HIV discovery era, I was always fascinated by the kind of lifestyle previous to that, and at the same time, felt repulsed by it to a certain degree. <br/><br/>The first chapters of this book are remarkable. Simply magnificent. The memories of childhood the narrator describes are so evocative and realistic it's unbelievable. Many quotes to remember and many things to delve into and take from. The language is the kind of language</span>
  <span id="freeText15442636149237090712" style="display:none">I wanted to like this book more - I really did. Having been born post the AIDS/HIV discovery era, I was always fascinated by the kind of lifestyle previous to that, and at the same time, felt repulsed by it to a certain degree. <br /><br />The first chapters of this book are remarkable. Simply magnificent. The memories of childhood the narrator describes are so evocative and realistic it's unbelievable. Many quotes to remember and many things to delve into and take from. The language is the kind of language I always loved - the language I wish I could manipulate the same way Rechy does. He can actually <i>touch</i> the words, feel them, weigh them, know their texture and taste. Rechy doesn't know what dashes or apostrophes mean, but that's exactly what sets him apart: he denies playing by the rules and he structures a whole different universe in which he is the king. Even if he tries too hard to prove he deserves the crown.<br /><br />But what didn't appeal to me in this book is the majority of the stories. I feel there are many we could have done without. The characters - despite their proximity to realism - feel contrite (today anyway) and don't make the gay community look beyond what the stereotypes for it say. It's depressing and heavy and a spoonful down your throat. I loved the beginning and ending sequences and the sentence which ends this book is simply brilliant. <br /><br />But at the end of the day, I think Rechy himself describes my feelings for this book in his last paragraphs: <i>And what has been found? Nothing. A circle which winds around, without beginning, without end.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="15442636149237090712" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_29693466" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/29693466" />
    <a title="Matthew Lawrence" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/300337-matthew-lawrence"><img alt="Matthew Lawrence" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455727135p2/300337.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/29693466?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer11590567166443775853">Amazingly overdone. It's one of those books that I read really, really slowly just because I didn't want it to be over. Emotionally I think it touched on a lot of stuff I related to (and haven't read about before), to the point where I was willing--happy, even--to overlook things like the description of a hot dog cart as a relic from Hell.</span>

<div id="review_75923873" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75923873" />
    <a title="Kerry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/941470-kerry"><img alt="Kerry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1217536302p2/941470.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75923873?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kerry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kerry" href="/user/show/941470-kerry">Kerry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75923873" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17532487778904080524">I gave it four stars... so you know I enjoyed it. But that doesn't mean I don't have a song and dance to tell you about it now. Let's commence shaking tailfeathers on this, but only one apiece. I don't want any injuries. <br/><br/>Now, let's... talk... GRAMMAR. It's a freaking important part of our language. It can change entire meanings of phrases and sentences. But there are those that like to give you that "I'm an artist and it's how I form my craft" line. When really it's turd. And you do NOT want to </span>
  <span id="freeText17532487778904080524" style="display:none">I gave it four stars... so you know I enjoyed it. But that doesn't mean I don't have a song and dance to tell you about it now. Let's commence shaking tailfeathers on this, but only one apiece. I don't want any injuries. <br /><br />Now, let's... talk... GRAMMAR. It's a freaking important part of our language. It can change entire meanings of phrases and sentences. But there are those that like to give you that "I'm an artist and it's how I form my craft" line. When really it's turd. And you do NOT want to see how some of us form that art. <br /><br />I digress. What I meant to say was that Senor Rechy walks the fine line of those that can pull this off and those straight blow it. I understand the stream of conscience writing style, I mean I'm from Kerouac's hometown for godsakes! But don't pussyfoot around it! You can't just whimsically use commas and apostrophes one second and throw them to the wind the next. Gotta pick a side of the fence homey. <br /><br />That being said, one very important note I'd like to bring attention to in this is that it is the first book I've ever read with the phrase "sunbleached pubic hair" in it... I know you're shocked that I haven't dug that one up before... I didn't say I hadn't WRITTEN the phrase, just that I hadn't read it elsewhere. Pick up those dropped jaws kids. Which is maybe why I give Mr. Rechy bonus points. <br /><br />OR, perhaps because I really enjoyed his language and prose throughout it. I may never have been a transient male prostitute in my life (yet), but something rings true and warm in my soul with such quotes as: "The heart is deceitful above all things". I'm not lying that is BRILLIANT. I honestly just may have it tattooed on me. His life may not be your cup of tea, but I guarantee that everyone finds a way to relate to the life lessons he learns.<br /><br />To the protagonist, the end of youth is a kind of death. And he spends his life running from that death. Held jobs, but the street lured him back every time. Sure he could work a 9 to 5... so why keep returning to the streets... for me it's crack, but for him, it's much more... it's that early-life crisis we all have after college and when we're supposed to settle down and marry and have a family. Except usually we just go to Vegas and get a lil buckwild and a lil over it. It's never so easy for everyone. But everyone wants a taste of that sinister life. It's their draw to Mardi Gras and Vegas. They just want a taste of the city of night but not to reside there. However, some people are just drawn to it like some are drawn to be artists or engineers. It's a trade those who aren't a part of don't understand. <br /><br />But then that all comes tumbling down when he's questioned why he does it by someone who knows all too well: to avoid his worst fear of having to love someone. I will admit that thought is horrifying. Just THINK of all the x-mas and anniversary and valentine's day gifts. Good god. Bloodsuckers. <br /><br />Digressing again, he's deathly afraid to place himself in a vulnerable situation... as I feel we all honestly are. You don't want to leave yourself exposed to hurt, but it's the only way you can truly fill that void of loneliness. Instead, he fills that need w people who want him, but only in brief interludes so to never chance having to reciprocate emotions.<br /><br />All and all this book carries much more weight than the premise of it seems to entail. Which is why I gave it four stars. But work on that grammar homey. I'm only letting it slide once before I hi-five your face. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17532487778904080524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

This book is basically a queer take on '<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road' title='The Road by Cormac McCarthy' rel='nofollow noopener'>On the Road</a>', featuring Rechy going around the big country hustling himself and writing about it in detail.   <span id="freeText15679527197443437457" style="display:none">I read this because David Bowie cited it as one of his favorite books in an interview, and Bowie's got some good taste (did you know "Wham, bam, thank you mam" is a reference to a Charles Mingus song?) This book is basically a queer take on '<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road" title="The Road by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">On the Road</a>', featuring Rechy going around the big country hustling himself and writing about it in detail. He's incredibly laid back and observational about the whole thing, whether he's trying to milk money off a rich client in Los Angeles or watching a drag queen kick the shit out of a heckler in New Orleans. Lou Reed definitely read this book at some point, since the lyrical sensibility matches up perfectly. Someone's going to make a movie out of this one day. </span>

<div id="review_1975197755" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1975197755" />
    <a title="Judy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3333943-judy"><img alt="Judy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267037102p2/3333943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1975197755?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Judy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Judy" href="/user/show/3333943-judy">Judy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=20th-century-fiction">20th-century-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=books-from-1963">books-from-1963</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1975197755" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5626761052583123553"><br/>Oh the places I go in My Big Fat Reading Project! At #7 on the 1963 bestseller list, this novel was a ground breaker in gay fiction. I had never heard of it but my cohort in the Literary Snobs reading group knew all about it. In some ways it was unlike anything I have ever read while in other ways it felt familiar compared to some of the Beat fiction I have read.<br/><br/>Largely autobiographical, the story follows a young man through his peripatetic nightlife as a hustler in the dark streets of El Paso, </span>
  <span id="freeText5626761052583123553" style="display:none"><br />Oh the places I go in My Big Fat Reading Project! At #7 on the 1963 bestseller list, this novel was a ground breaker in gay fiction. I had never heard of it but my cohort in the Literary Snobs reading group knew all about it. In some ways it was unlike anything I have ever read while in other ways it felt familiar compared to some of the Beat fiction I have read.<br /><br />Largely autobiographical, the story follows a young man through his peripatetic nightlife as a hustler in the dark streets of El Paso, Times Square, Pershing Square (in Los Angeles), the French Quarter of New Orleans, and the Mission District of San Francisco. His lonely, frenetic existence is portrayed as a search for identity and connection but the milieu in which he searches is a desperate world of disconnection and confused identities, made up of hustlers, queens, secret slumming homosexual men, and sad women, a few of whom are lesbians. In fact, there are few women in the book and those few seemed like stereotypes. <br /><br />What was brought home to me is how horrid life could be for gay people in mid-twentieth century America. So much secrecy, confusion, guilt and personal disintegration was necessarily their plight. Though the endless rehashing of similar scenes got to be too much for me as a reader, I wondered if life is much better for gay persons in our present time.<br /><br />I can't really know because I am heterosexual and though I have gay friends there is a reticence between us when it comes to talking about sexual orientation. Is that my doing? I want to ask about or discuss what that aspect of their lives is like but I feel shy about doing so. I was raised to be homophobic and confess that it took some doing to get over that prejudice. Mostly, it took reading books.<br /><br />So I thank the writers who have opened their hearts and minds in their novels and memoirs, their essays and poetry. I don't mean to sound disingenuous, but like someone who hated a certain food as a kid but grew up to like it, I can barely remember what it was like to harbor that homophobia in the past. Yet, I feel more comfortable with olives than I do with my fellow human beings whose sexual orientation is different from mine.<br /><br />In any case, <i>City of Night</i> was a wild, sometimes uncomfortable read that brought me more understanding of what is means to be human. It also made me sorrow for the ways we mistreat each other. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5626761052583123553" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_358043960" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/358043960" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Gallaway" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Gallaway" href="/user/show/1768055-matthew-gallaway">Matthew Gallaway</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer358043960" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2613338106592653502">This book should be required reading for anyone interested in 1950s/60s "underground culture" (for lack of a better term, or sort of the opposite of straight, white, heteronormative Mad Men). The book is about a mostly gay hustler who drifts through the major U.S. cities and in the process manages to dissect quite a few stereotypes that are still very pervasive on the gender/sexuality front and also manages to invent a new language to describe what feels like a new world. Lonely, punishing, and </span>
  <span id="freeText2613338106592653502" style="display:none">This book should be required reading for anyone interested in 1950s/60s "underground culture" (for lack of a better term, or sort of the opposite of straight, white, heteronormative Mad Men). The book is about a mostly gay hustler who drifts through the major U.S. cities and in the process manages to dissect quite a few stereotypes that are still very pervasive on the gender/sexuality front and also manages to invent a new language to describe what feels like a new world. Lonely, punishing, and transcendent. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2613338106592653502" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3343011653" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6778906511043551715">When I picked this book to write about this morning, I noticed that a lot of my friends have written about it, which is natural, since this is an iconic gay male novel, born out of an era in which writing of this blunt honesty was nearly impossible. This is the kind of book that Grove Press was known for, edgy and hard. It is also the kind of book that people speak of as hard, edgy, frank, which means that the writer treats sex in a particular way; at least I find that to be true most of the tim</span>
  <span id="freeText6778906511043551715" style="display:none">When I picked this book to write about this morning, I noticed that a lot of my friends have written about it, which is natural, since this is an iconic gay male novel, born out of an era in which writing of this blunt honesty was nearly impossible. This is the kind of book that Grove Press was known for, edgy and hard. It is also the kind of book that people speak of as hard, edgy, frank, which means that the writer treats sex in a particular way; at least I find that to be true most of the time. There are not a lot of parallels to this book; it is reminiscent of other writers who deal directly with erotic matter, but it is born out of one man's hard-won experience. The ideas about gay sex that Rechy wrote about fell out of fashion in the aftermath of HIV; he was an advocate for hustler sex, anonymous sex, public sex; the world he writes about is that night-driven world that gay people occupied in that same era. It is a hard era for people to comprehend and is easy to dismiss as a time when men were closeted and full of self hatred. The fact that the closets and the self hatred were the natural outgrowth of trying to find intimacy in the shadows and corners of the world is harder to see. Rechy does not write about those ideas. He simply presents the world of the hustler, the world of the drag queen, the scenes of gay bars in New Orleans, with the sensibility of a natural inhabit of all these milieus. He does so with force and brilliance and a good strong dose of messiness. The writing is extravagant, self-involved, and true. It's almost pointless to call this book a classic; it is a singular novel that only got itself born because of the force of the writer and his unswerving vision. He wrote many other novels, most of them not successful; but a few of them are essential - Numbers and The Sexual Outlaw come immediately to mind. Much as I love this book, I have never been able to face rereading it. I suppose I don't want to spoil my first impression of it, when everything Rechy had to say was vital to my understanding of myself.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6778906511043551715" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1255283803" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1255283803" />
    <a title="Chris Muscardin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27908087-chris-muscardin"><img alt="Chris Muscardin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576730016p2/27908087.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1255283803?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Muscardin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Muscardin" href="/user/show/27908087-chris-muscardin">Chris Muscardin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780394621692" href="/book/show/3823752-city-of-night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27908087-chris-muscardin?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1255283803" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14643906152128399861">What a massive, sprawling, and exhausting (in the best possible way) novel. This is one of those which will pick you up, spin you around, chew you up and spit you back out.<br/><br/>I'm unsure how in my collection of gay literature canon I somehow glossed over this novel until now. I'm glad that in this year's endeavor to "re-read my mothers" of gay literature a friend made me pick it up. <i>City of Night</i> follows an unnamed hustler as he bounces through the queer underworlds of America's so-called "cities of</span>
  <span id="freeText14643906152128399861" style="display:none">What a massive, sprawling, and exhausting (in the best possible way) novel. This is one of those which will pick you up, spin you around, chew you up and spit you back out.<br /><br />I'm unsure how in my collection of gay literature canon I somehow glossed over this novel until now. I'm glad that in this year's endeavor to "re-read my mothers" of gay literature a friend made me pick it up. <i>City of Night</i> follows an unnamed hustler as he bounces through the queer underworlds of America's so-called "cities of night": New York, LA, San Francisco, Chicago, New Orleans. Written in a Beatnik-esque stream-of-conciousness that at first feels a bit like affectation, the distinctive voice develops into an accurate syntactical depiction of our restless narrator, who is always searching, searching, <i>searching</i> for the indefinable something, called sometimes love, sometimes desire, sometimes validation of existence. While the places he speaks of so intimately may have aged away (I believe a good among of the account was drawn from Rechy's own youth), the scenes he describes and the palpable emotion never will.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14643906152128399861" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13730220293617135947" style="display:none">Bits of this book are certainly 4-star, a few chapters may even merit 5, but the overall journey through the nocturnal world of 1960s America's gay hustling scene, over almost 400 pages of small print, was just a little too arduous for my liking. The largely passive narrator acts as a device for hearing out the stories of various characters from this furtive sexual underworld, a few of which are incredibly poignant; but there are longueurs too across the many months and miles, and any resolution of the central character's emotional gridlock caused by craving and fearing love in equal measures is only hinted at by the end.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2708108327742338844">I actually finished this book months ago, but I never rated it because I wanted to write a comprehensive review and now it was so long ago that all i can say:<br/><br/>1. It was amazing.<br/></span>

<div id="review_2944713229" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2944713229" />
    <a title="Seth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/74992809-seth"><img alt="Seth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513339232p2/74992809.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2944713229?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seth" href="/user/show/74992809-seth">Seth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802121530" href="/book/show/16129339-city-of-night">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2944713229" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11414500084347756302">In the late 1950s, John Rechy returned to his hometown of El Paso, TX, after several years spent criss-crossing the US. Shortly after arriving, he recounted his experiences in a manic letter to a friend. The letter was never sent; instead, it became the staring point for <i>City of Night</i>.<br/><br/>Reading the novel now, it's difficult to imagine the scandal it caused upon publication. The unnamed narrator survives by hustling other men - although the sex scenes are so non-explicit, it's not always clear what</span>
  <span id="freeText11414500084347756302" style="display:none">In the late 1950s, John Rechy returned to his hometown of El Paso, TX, after several years spent criss-crossing the US. Shortly after arriving, he recounted his experiences in a manic letter to a friend. The letter was never sent; instead, it became the staring point for <i>City of Night</i>.<br /><br />Reading the novel now, it's difficult to imagine the scandal it caused upon publication. The unnamed narrator survives by hustling other men - although the sex scenes are so non-explicit, it's not always clear what's going on. (Generally, the guys seem to be sucking his dick, at $10 a pop. Not bad bank for the 1950s, I guess.)<br /><br />Needless to say, the narrator meets some colorful characters during his travels, from closet cases and drag queens to fortune tellers and S/M enthusiasts. As other reviewers point out, the novel has a sort of documentary quality, showing us the sexual underground of the 1950s and name-checking prominent gay (Rock Hudson, Tab Hunter) and camp (Tallaluh Bankhead, Bette Davis) figures of the time.<br /><br />The book certainly has some memorable scenes - particularly the night the narrator spends with another hustler named Pete. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(They clearly want each other but are so afraid of admitting their queerness they ultimately do nothing more than hold hands.) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I can also see the influence the book's had on other texts, from <i>The Basketball Diaries</i> to <i>The Rules of Attraction</i> to <i>Slouching Toward Bethlehem</i>. (The opening paragraph of part 2 could easily have been written by Joan Didion herself.)<br /> <br />Having said all of that, I have to admit the novel falls a little flat. First, because of the narrator's long-winded self-analyses (his mother loved him too much, his father didn't love him enough, etc.) Second, because of Rechy's decision to ditch the manic energy of his original letter and to write a series of detailed vignettes instead, some of which go on and on...<br /><br />Capturing characters' voices is a challenge for any writer. <i>City of Night</i> has some well-done moments in this regard; see, especially, the long list of stories told about Miss Destiny (she died, she's in prison, she got married, she went straight...), a drag queen who suddenly disappears from the scene. But on the whole I found the book kind of exhausting, and while I'm glad I finally read it, I doubt I'll want to read it again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11414500084347756302" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2497398752" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2497398752" />
    <a title="Matthew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20910065-matthew"><img alt="Matthew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587674435p2/20910065.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2497398752?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew" href="/user/show/20910065-matthew">Matthew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2497398752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10340921137121240866">Like many future English majors/wannabe-writers, I was obsessed with postmodern lit between the ages of 17 and 21 – most especially anything associated with the Beat Generation. And while John Rechy’s landmark debut, City of Night, shares many similarities with the Beats that popularized their iconic movement (and the novels that defined it), it managed to fly under my radar. How? Better still, why? I only hope the various lit electives I took as a budding creative writer have since revised thei</span>
  <span id="freeText10340921137121240866" style="display:none">Like many future English majors/wannabe-writers, I was obsessed with postmodern lit between the ages of 17 and 21 – most especially anything associated with the Beat Generation. And while John Rechy’s landmark debut, City of Night, shares many similarities with the Beats that popularized their iconic movement (and the novels that defined it), it managed to fly under my radar. How? Better still, why? I only hope the various lit electives I took as a budding creative writer have since revised their syllabi to include Rechy’s debut. I’d be saddened if it weren’t being taught or least talked about, yet hopeful the generation below me were to learn of it sooner than myself. <br /><br />That said I also wonder if I would’ve deemed City of Night the “greatest book ever” had I read it in college. After all, it checks every box that 1998-me aimed to check: quests for spiritual liberation; narratives celebrating un-apologetic hedonism; gorgeous, explosive, free-flowing prose that entrances as much as it meanders. Truth be told, reading City of Night at 41 had me feeling nostalgic not for the time which it documents, but for the one which I devoured any and everything like it. <br /><br />Rechy paints an urban landscape made up of misfits and addicts, prostitutes and drag queens, that’s rich and vivid and altogether otherworldly. We follow a “youngman” huster from his El Paso roots to both coasts and seemingly everywhere in between, where he all but literally walks on the wild side. He becomes rapt within the gay nightlife, wherein he both connects with several fellow homosexual men, and also prostitutes himself to them in order to make ends meet. <br /><br />Comparisons will be made to Kerouac’s seminal On the Road, and rightfully so. Both works document young dreamers venturing their way across the country, the eclectic souls they encounter (physically and spiritually). Yet where On the Road takes an abstract if not surreal approach, City of Night is more streamlined, more focused. That’s not to say Rechy’s prose does not have a flair for the dramatic, as demonstrated in several glorious passages that almost read like poetry. Yet the author always manages to reel things in, not let things get too awry – quite a feat given the chaotic subject matter. <br /><br />It’s this subject matter that’s likely why City of Night had all but passed me by. It puts the novel in a difficult class; several classes, in fact: road journal; queer fiction; Beat narrative; urban fantasy. Most of all, City of Night is the work of a pioneering voice, one so resounding it paved the way for not one but several genres. I only hope it continues to get its due; I’m thankful to say it finally has mine. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10340921137121240866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_948610048" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/948610048" />
    <a title="Allan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25891345-allan"><img alt="Allan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522446840p2/25891345.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/948610048?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Allan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Allan" href="/user/show/25891345-allan">Allan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802121530" href="/book/show/16129339-city-of-night">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer948610048" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6601930085993951463">I picked up the 50th anniversary copy of this novel in The Strand in NYC based on its blurb, it somehow having passed under my radar in the past. Telling the story of a male hustler in pre Stonewall USA, the book was a Bestseller on release, despite coming in for criticism from many reviewers at the time. <br/><br/>As the main character moves from El Paso to NYC, before taking in cities like LA, Dallas, Chicago and SF, we read stories about the 'dive bars', 'scores', 'queens' and 'hustlers' he encounters,</span>
  <span id="freeText6601930085993951463" style="display:none">I picked up the 50th anniversary copy of this novel in The Strand in NYC based on its blurb, it somehow having passed under my radar in the past. Telling the story of a male hustler in pre Stonewall USA, the book was a Bestseller on release, despite coming in for criticism from many reviewers at the time. <br /><br />As the main character moves from El Paso to NYC, before taking in cities like LA, Dallas, Chicago and SF, we read stories about the 'dive bars', 'scores', 'queens' and 'hustlers' he encounters, some of these which are easier to follow than others. Things culminate for him at Mardi Gras in New Orleans, with a moment of clarity arriving in the midst of the debauchery alluded to.<br /><br />I can't say that I particularly enjoyed this book, but I was interested in the makeup of the world it portrayed from a social history point of view. While I didn't identify with any of the characters, I've no doubt that in the unenlightened times when the book was written, many people had to live in the cloak and dagger fashion recorded, and were irrevocably damaged as a result. I'm just thankful that the world has moved on in the way it has, and while no doubt the darker elements of the novel still probably exist, they're not the only option.<br /><br />All in all, not a particularly easy read, but an interesting one if you're into seeing the changes in both LGBT lifestyles and indeed LGBT fiction since the 1960s.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6601930085993951463" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1230034092" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1230034092" />
    <a title="Bill Wallace" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39396037-bill-wallace"><img alt="Bill Wallace" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422540089p2/39396037.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1230034092?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Wallace" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Wallace" href="/user/show/39396037-bill-wallace">Bill Wallace</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1230034092" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13356732467259236058">At its best when it is nightmarish, John Rechy's classic account of hustling in the occluded world of homosexuality in mid-century America makes a compelling case that sexual identity is a spectrum rather than points on a plane. There are also at least two books here -- one an almost journalistic account of life in the bars and on the streets and another, much less satisfying overlay of drama and unconvincing introspection. Rechy's prose works well when it's sparse but all too often it veers int</span>
  <span id="freeText13356732467259236058" style="display:none">At its best when it is nightmarish, John Rechy's classic account of hustling in the occluded world of homosexuality in mid-century America makes a compelling case that sexual identity is a spectrum rather than points on a plane. There are also at least two books here -- one an almost journalistic account of life in the bars and on the streets and another, much less satisfying overlay of drama and unconvincing introspection. Rechy's prose works well when it's sparse but all too often it veers into the purple, as contemporary critics so unkindly noted. Still, the world portrayed here -- and read about by people who would never have dreamed it existed -- creates characters and situations that fuel stacks of later novels and hours of film. I read the book because of my endless fascination with outsider culture, especially in ages of great conformity. Two chapters at the end of the third book, one about the man who initiates our narrator into the world of S&amp;M and one about a "talent contest" at a bar in Chicago, are among the best horror stories I've read lately. The Mardi Gras scenes are also genuinely Boschian and more than repaid the effort of getting through some of the talkier chapters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13356732467259236058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_828116519" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/828116519" />
    <a title="Rachel Pollock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5277674-rachel-pollock"><img alt="Rachel Pollock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442879493p2/5277674.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/828116519?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel Pollock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel Pollock" href="/user/show/5277674-rachel-pollock">Rachel Pollock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer828116519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17561887500683745986">I read this book because it was recommended (posthumously, in a long-ago interview) by David Bowie. I have such complicated feelings about it, but i ultimately rated it four stars because even though it depressed the shit out of me by the time i finally made it to the end, i also found so much of it to be familiar in terms of the speech and behavior of the people in it. It's a thinly-veiled roman a clef about the author's life as a rough-trade hustler in the gay scenes of several major US cities</span>
  <span id="freeText17561887500683745986" style="display:none">I read this book because it was recommended (posthumously, in a long-ago interview) by David Bowie. I have such complicated feelings about it, but i ultimately rated it four stars because even though it depressed the shit out of me by the time i finally made it to the end, i also found so much of it to be familiar in terms of the speech and behavior of the people in it. It's a thinly-veiled roman a clef about the author's life as a rough-trade hustler in the gay scenes of several major US cities in the 1950s, and for something which is now over 50 years old, it still felt really familiar, like these could be people i knew at a gay bar in the 1990s or even 2000s. But it's also so so so so fucking depressing, the way in which everyone in the book pretty much is a walking wounded tragedy, trying to exist in a culture that hates and demonizes them just for their sexuality and/or gender expression. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17561887500683745986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2110806180" />
    <a title="Megan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/408843-megan"><img alt="Megan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1390876833p2/408843.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2110806180?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megan" href="/user/show/408843-megan">Megan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/408843-megan?shelf=queer">queer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/408843-megan?shelf=macsulinity-studies">macsulinity-studies</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2110806180" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9834191104796681443">Epic and meticulous lightly fictionalized study of gay night life and the hustler lifestyle in the 50s and 60s -- big cast of memorable characters, gorgeous and unsettling writing, concretely structured (in interesting tension with the sprawl of so many scenes), hardened yet tender protag. Took me forever to read but I loved dipping into this world a little bit at a time. <br/><br/>Tons of transmisogyny -- this novel is really a study in that too, in the ways in which it documents (somewhat anthropologica</span>
  <span id="freeText9834191104796681443" style="display:none">Epic and meticulous lightly fictionalized study of gay night life and the hustler lifestyle in the 50s and 60s -- big cast of memorable characters, gorgeous and unsettling writing, concretely structured (in interesting tension with the sprawl of so many scenes), hardened yet tender protag. Took me forever to read but I loved dipping into this world a little bit at a time. <br /><br />Tons of transmisogyny -- this novel is really a study in that too, in the ways in which it documents (somewhat anthropologically) gay/drag/trans tensions and femmephobia within the gay community. These in-community aggressions are not as carefully criticized as they are in, say, Giovanni's Room (published 7 years earlier), though the narrator is more observant and curious, and much less judgmental than David in G's Room.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9834191104796681443" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Simon Hollway" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27637045-simon-hollway"><img alt="Simon Hollway" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1426912194p2/27637045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1221577351?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon Hollway" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon Hollway" href="/user/show/27637045-simon-hollway">Simon Hollway</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1221577351" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15661563769172749083">Set before my time but I was blown away by the language, the ghetto pattois, the polari, call it what you will. That cool, hip and wantonly slick and sleek flow of words, I had never encountered before. What a loss! Much of the vernacular did fill in some missing blanks and made me understand many terms still floating about on the modern scene. Wow! Such beautiful, evocative dialogue. Never fear though. That slacker mentality is thankfully back in its mischievously muted manner - 'It gave me the</span>
  <span id="freeText15661563769172749083" style="display:none">Set before my time but I was blown away by the language, the ghetto pattois, the polari, call it what you will. That cool, hip and wantonly slick and sleek flow of words, I had never encountered before. What a loss! Much of the vernacular did fill in some missing blanks and made me understand many terms still floating about on the modern scene. Wow! Such beautiful, evocative dialogue. Never fear though. That slacker mentality is thankfully back in its mischievously muted manner - 'It gave me the feels' being one of my current favourites. City of Night should be enshrined in a time capsule and revisited every 20 years by successive generations.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15661563769172749083" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2312936419" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2312936419" />
    <a title="Patrick Hackett" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5167936-patrick-hackett"><img alt="Patrick Hackett" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521028649p2/5167936.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2312936419?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick Hackett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick Hackett" href="/user/show/5167936-patrick-hackett">Patrick Hackett</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2312936419" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6314407920525491342">I wanted to like this more than I actually did. Rechy does a beautiful - and I mean stunningly beautiful - job painting a vivid picture of the cities and the gay underground culture of the 1960's that the protagonist inhabited. But beyond that I found there to be so much psychobabble and pretension that the book was at times completely insufferable. I'm glad I read it since it's a gay classic, but would not read again.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2485047871270297886">I read this book and it's extremely overrated, drags on for way too long and should have been edited more, and Rechy's narcissism and snobbery shows through the fiction. There are better Rechy books out there such as Numbers. This book is not one of his best, and unfortunately people fluff it as though it is his best, and most people do not read any of his other novels.</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('IOPusSQRxdluusZ3ruylD18FXIyWpVdK6dGn/qYMZAH09nCSdfzOdhmk1XJKOx+XHiMVxP0BQ1WV3LjWGtFclQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('653hYGQEMNE9/OlRWd3OzCYyzBRPFqXcj8xDM2iCSTE/iH9DNek7fkri+lS9CnRUZxSFXCSyscPzwVwb1F9xpQ==')})
  }

What most impressed the first readers of this memoir, however, was the candor with which he describes his life in gay Berlin of the 1930s and his struggles to save his companion, a German man named Heinz, from the Nazis. An engrossing and dramatic story and a fascinating glimpse into a little-known world, Christopher and His Kind remains one of Isherwood\'s greatest achievements. A major figure in twentieth-century fiction and the gay rights movement, Christopher Isherwood (1904-1986) is the author of Down There on a Visit, Lions and Shadows, A Meeting by the River, The             var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

</li>
</ul>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/29414.John_Rechy">About John Rechy</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/29414.John_Rechy"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1221855021p3/29414.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/29414.John_Rechy">John Rechy
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
140
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2594796286102313424">John Rechy is an American author, the child of a Scottish father and a Mexican-American mother. In his novels he has written extensively about homosexual culture in Los Angeles and wider America, and is among the pioneers of modern LGBT literature. Drawing on his own background, he has also contributed to Chicano literature, especially with his novel The Miraculous Day of Amalia Gomez, which is ta</span>
  <span id="freeText2594796286102313424" style="display:none">John Rechy is an American author, the child of a Scottish father and a Mexican-American mother. In his novels he has written extensively about homosexual culture in Los Angeles and wider America, and is among the pioneers of modern LGBT literature. Drawing on his own background, he has also contributed to Chicano literature, especially with his novel The Miraculous Day of Amalia Gomez, which is taught in several Chicano literature courses in the United States. His work has often faced censorship due to its sexual content, particularly (but not solely) in the 1960s and 1970s, but books such as City of Night have been best sellers, and he has many literary admirers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2594796286102313424" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
